B0052AC8C6|5.0|I admit that I might have rated Rango as only four stars if not to compensate for the many misguided reviews of those longing for insincere love songs, obligatory romances, and energy drinks. This film is not for them. Rango is art, as all films should be. It is meant to stimulate the human desire for poetry and a supernatural understanding of the world. That may sound dramatic, but thoughtful children's films are so precious, and Rango is one.<br /><br />I fully expected, as most did, to witness another mindless drudge of a children's animation in the same vein as Pixar's Finding Nemo, but thank God for film makers who use their brains! The dialog shines as the characters share insightful idioms and clever references. At no point is there a wasted line of dialog. Most of what is said will go over the heads of young children - and clearly adults who never read - but there is enough silly humor to incite genuine laughter. Adults will gasp giddily at the sharp innuendo and provocative language, but again, it is subtle enough that children will only know that something humorous was said. If you aren't receptive to the steady stream of wit that pours from each word, then, yes, you very well may be bored because much of the film is carried by John Logan's fascinating script. The plot leaves much to be desired, and is merely used as a backdrop for some abstract philosophical concepts and the atypical delivery of children's entertainment. It didn't bother me.<br /><br />The animation is outstanding, but more than that, the imagery is full of expression. The film's charm is elevated by crisp animation and a deliberate art direction. The feel of Rango is unique, and may come across as edgy, but the universe that is inhabited by charming creatures is far more convincing than what films like Avatar or Monsters Inc. even came close to achieving.<br /><br />The creators of Rango meant to make a children's feature that was more than just a cute story about the power of love and friendship; they wanted to make something that actually mattered. The problem is, a lot of people can't grasp the necessity for enriching every aspect of life so that we can all broaden our understanding a littler wider. As a child, I would have appreciated the magnitude of what the film makers were attempting to accomplish, and the world could do better if all children were the same. It saddens me that so many adults who find their way onto Amazon.com are so resistant to what artists are trying to achieve for the human race. If you found that you weren't in-tuned to the higher meaning of the film, then you should probably put down 50 Shades of Grey and pick up Charles Dickens.

Overall, I would say that I liked this book, but it was certainly not among his best works.<br /><br />First, I thought the new characters brought out in this second book were fairly well phased in...there were probably several instances in the separate stories that will make you feel that the individual stories are a little too hoaky. I found that I liked most of the new main characters, but I seemed to have missed what happened to some of the old characters...such as Walter's parents...I will go back and see if I skipped over that.  Some of the decisions made by the different characters made me scratch my head in wonder, but I guess you have to allow some leeway for story direction. You can see that one of the problems with this book is that you have to make a lot of allowances which is not typical of his (Follett's) other books.  I think that I have to agree with some of the other reviewers in that if this had not been a Ken Follett book, I might not have stuck it out to the end.<br /><br />I was a little disappointed that the author apparently felt the need to tag my Navy as the gay branch. 1609613937|1.0|As an academic researcher and an avid follower of nutrition science, I was excited to buy this book and to read about (as the book description told me) the scientifically-based rebuttals it made to contemporary \\"fad\\" high protein diets. I found nothing of the sort within the pages of this book.<br /><br />Rather, what I found was a series of unfounded statements and faulty logic. Many of the authors' most important arguments about how our ancestors ate or about how we should be eating today cite either bad/outdated science, websites(!), or nothing at all. The personally-motivated stance of the authors makes it hard to determine which evidence is credible and which isn't.<br /><br />There are also innumerable highly misleading (and in many occasions just plain wrong) \\"facts\\" that the authors use to try to establish the superiority of starch over protein. Many of these fly in the face of established research. I'll name just a couple. In comparing the caloric profile of \\"starch\\" vs. \\"meat\\", they state that starch has 1 calorie per gram and that \\"meat\\" has 4. The authors of course give no definition of what these starch or meat products actually are. Their caloric profile could be true if you compare a yam to a fatty piece of bacon, but I can just as easily make the opposite claim: a skinless chicken breast has or piece of fish has less than 1 calorie per gram whereas wheat flour has about 3.5 calories per gram. They also show a pie chart that implies that a typical american's diet consists of 70% meat and dairy (whether they mean as a percent of calories or total foodstuff they don't indicate). Not surprisingly, they cite nothing for this highly inaccurate figure; from my understanding less than a third of American's calories come from animal protein and dairy, and the majority (50%+) come from added fats and carbohydrates. The authors also list protein and fat as \\"toxins\\"... just one of the many scientifically irresponsible statements in the book.<br /><br />Although there are several good arguments to be leveled against contemporary diets and high-protein diets, you won't find any novel ones in this book. B006G3MZRQ|3.0|Warrior is one of the few movies I heard nothing but good things about last year. Most of the people I know with similar tastes to me praised it and put it on their 'best of 2011' lists within the top 10. This surprised me since I'm not used to sports movies getting such great feedback. Sure, I like a lot of sport movies, but I've never seen one that really made me want to go back and watch it again, let alone put it on a top 10, 25, 50 list. I recently gave Warrior a shot once it became available on a certain movie viewing service and, while it held my interest for the most part, it didn't win me over. The movie has some outstanding performances from Joel Edgerton, Tom Hardy and Nick Nolte, and some fairly good fight sequences, but for me there was one big flaw, and that was one of the main characters. Warrior is still definitely worth watching- I don't even like MMA or the UFC and here I am recommending it.<br /><br />We start out with a young man, Tommy Conlon coming back to his hometown and meeting up with his dad, Paddy. This is a huge surprise to Paddy, as he hasn't seen his son in ages. A lot's happened since they last saw each other- Paddy's been sober for almost 1000 days, and now Tommy's looking to start training again. Unfortunately, Tommy lacks the decency to not drink in front of his old man, tempting him with it now and then. Even with this temptation, Paddy takes him in once again. We then meet Brendan, a high school physics teacher who's on the verge of losing his home to the bank. He refuses to file for bankruptcy and resorts to fighting in a strip club parking lot with local guys for a little extra money. Brendan's wife doesn't know about this, as he says he's just working as a bouncer. His luck gets even worse when the school finds out about his fighting, and suspends him without pay for a month. Meanwhile, Tommy becomes an instant internet sensation after a clip of him knocking out a local big shot gets uploaded. With constant training and fights, he's headed for a big event where the grand prize is five million dollars. Brendan, a former UFC competitor, also finds out about the tournament, and we soon learn that he and Tommy are brothers.<br /><br />...can you guess where this is going?<br /><br />I was pleasantly surprised by the fact that Warrior wasn't just some dumb fight-fight-fight movie, focusing on dudes getting beat up for 2 hours. The training sequences are well-done, and the beginning fights are over quickly, showing just how fast someone can be taken down, regardless of size or experience. Brendan's past his prime, and the guys he fights aren't exactly on the same page as him, so he's got a ways to go if he's to catch up with Tommy, a former wrestling star and Marine. Of the two characters, I wanted Brendan to win the tournament right from the get-go. He cares about his family having a good home, as well as their financial future. I cared about this character because he's just plain likable. Sure, five million bucks may be a heck of a lot more than he needs for his home situation, but hey, why NOT five million? Tommy on the other hand...well, here's the thing that got me with this movie. I read a lot of comments about both men being underdogs, and viewers not being able to figure out who they wanted to win, because both characters deserved to win. There's no doubting the latter there, but I could not stand Tommy. Arrogant, lacking respect for his father outside of using him as a coach again, not giving Brendan's family a chance when he shows Tommy pictures of his kids, there wasn't anything I liked about him except for one thing. Early on, we find out that he was a military hero, saving a group of his fellow Marines by himself, and also that his best friend was killed. Tommy is now trying to help out his friend's widow and children any way he can, and he wants to win the tournament and give them all of the money. That's a very noble thing to do, and in that sense, I couldn't help but -kind of-want him to win the tournament, but that was only if Brendan didn't come through. The family seems to be doing fine as it is: they're not in some rundown home and struggling for food or anything.<br /><br />Nick Notle does a phenomenal job as their father, never going over the top with his performance. The last thing I saw him in was Peaceful Warrior (how ironic), and I just might like his character here more but in a different way. Whenever alcohol would be near him, I'd keep thinking \\"don't do it!\\". The guy's come a long way, and had a tough time dealing with things over the years...it'd be a shame for him to throw away all that hard work. Getting chewed out by both of his boys wasn't any easier to see. A conversation between him and Tommy, where Tommy says, \\"where were you when it mattered? I needed this guy back when I was a kid. I don't need you now. It's too late now. Everything's already happened\\" was especially painful to see and hear, partially because I feel the same way about my dad.<br /><br />Even at the end of this review now, I'm debating on if I should bump the rating up to 4 stars. Warrior moves along quickly and never feels like it's 2 hours and 20 minutes. There aren't any wasted scenes or annoyingly drawn out shots, fights look very realistic, and Joel Edgerton as Brendan won me over. I just wish Tommy were more fleshed out and the plot weren't so predictable. Warrior's a great weekend choice, or just something to watch if you're sick of watching Rocky for the umpteenth time.

B006BAEQB2|5.0|Anne Neville (1456-1485) is not well-known as a personality in her own right. She is most famous for being the wronged wife of Richard III (1452-1485). At the time of her death, many whispered that she'd been poisoned by her husband to make way for his marriage to his niece Elizabeth of York (1466-1503). That marriage never happened, because Richard was obliged to deny before parliament that he'd had a relationship with Elizabeth. Shortly, thereafter on August 22, Richard was killed at the Battle of Bosworth Field.<br /><br />Anne is also known as the daughter of Richard Neville, Earl of Warwick (1429-1471), aka \\"Warwick the Kingmaker\\" for his making and unmaking of England's kings during the Wars of the Roses. And lastly, she is known for being the wife of Edward, Prince of Wales (1453-1471), the son and heir of Henry VI and Marguerite d'Anjou. They married in 1470, when Anne was only 14. Edward was killed at the Battle of Tewkesbury in 1471, leaving Anne a widow at the age of 15.<br /><br />So it was a pleasure to read Philippa Gregory's account of her in THE KINGMAKER'S DAUGHTER, in which Anne is the protagonist. In Ms. Gregory's account, Anne emerges as an endearing and intelligent heroine, caught up in the snares of her father's politics during as he swings from one side to another. Originally promised to Richard of Gloucester, a younger brother of the Yorkist King Edward IV, Anne is married off to Edward, Prince of Wales of the House of Lancaster. When Edward regains his throne and the House of Lancaster is crushed, Anne (according to Ms. Gregory) chooses to marry Richard of Gloucester as her second husband. Thus her marriage to the man who later becomes King Richard III, starts out as a love match.<br /><br />Ms. Gregory keeps very close to her characters in her stories, and I think this is what makes them so popular. In this novel, you feel as if you are actually with Anne as the events of her life unfold. The author has also done a superlative job with Anne's voice. Anne is not a charismatic person like Elizabeth Woodville or Jacquetta de St. Pol. Her voice does not have the bite of a Margaret Beaufort. Nevertheless, her quiet determination shines in this novel. 1416594795|5.0|Richard Dawkins' book \\"The Greatest Show on Earth: The Evidence for Evolution\\" is a thorough treatment of evolution for the casual reader.  The book traces out the various scientific arguments in favor of evolution, roughly corresponding to the following:<br /><br />1. Evolution's support as a theory (in the same way that gravity is a theory) in the scientific community<br />2. The existence of unnatural selection such as domestication and breeding of livestock<br />3. How natural selection is an extension of unnatural selection<br />4. Various geological dating techniques confirm the age of fossils<br />5. Observable evolution of organisms such as bacteria<br />6. The fossil record gives evidence of many transitional species<br />7. Likewise, the fossil record includes mankind's early descendants<br />8. Evidence from embryology<br />9. Evidence from islands and plate tectonics<br />10. Evidence from the similar features of evolutionary \\"cousins\\"<br />11. Evidence from genetics<br />12. Reasons that creationism is extremely unlikely<br /><br />This list does not exhaust the arguments that Dawkins makes, but rather gives an idea of what this book is about. He writes clearly and at an understandable level for non-scientists.  Every idea is explained clearly using relatable examples and a clear explanation of difficult science.  Many of the ideas in this book are complicated and it is to Dawkins' credit that he is able to convey his ideas so well.<br /><br />Many people will dislike this book for a number of reasons. For some it is that it conflicts with their beliefs, some will be put-off by Dawkins' uncompromising tone, and others just won't enjoy the topic.  If you think you fall into one of these categories, then you probably won't like this book and can without shame avoid reading it.  If on the other hand, you are curious about evolution, interested in challenging your biases and beliefs, and want to read something both challenging and increasingly relevant fortoday's world, I recommend this book without reservation.

B006ZT47O0|5.0|I've been listening to RFTI for a while now and I have to say that, while my first impression of the album was not the best, after about a week or so it really did start to grow on me. I think a lot of the other reviewers here probably never got over their same initial reactions as well, and that's a terrible shame because once you get past the adjustment period, RFTI is actually probably Epica's best album to date.<br /><br />The thing you have to understand about RFTI, and probably its biggest hurdle with the audience, is that it is Epica's first album without a catchy, conventional single to help ease listeners into the album. There's no \\"Unleashed,\\" there's no \\"Cry for the Moon\\", there's no \\"Never Enough\\". The single they threw at us was \\"Storm the Sorrow\\", which was probably picked because it was the most \\"conventional,\\" i.e. lacking in Mark's death growls. This should give you a pretty good idea of how steep of a curve the rest of the album is. I have a feeling that at this particular point in their careers, the folks in Epica had figured that they no longer need the hand-holding of catchy, poppy singles to help sell to their fans, and that they were going to do their own thing and we were just going to have to deal with it.<br /><br />What eventually broke through for me after about a day or two was \\"Monopoly on Truth.\\" It had a bit of everything--soaring highs, pounding lows, and layers upon layers of melodic complexity. \\"Serenade of Self-Destruction\\" came next for me for much of the same reasons. Both of these songs brought me back to the musical ideas of \\"Consigned to Oblivion,\\" where there's this big epic score broken up into a couple of movements with all these different elements coming together in a strangely jarring but ultimately cohesive musical narrative. Then the \\"Single-act\\" tracks followed shortly--\\"Deter the Tyrant\\" and \\"Avalanche\\" for me were the other big standouts, although none of the tracks on the album could be described as actually bad. Icould put this album on repeat by itself and leave it on all day without getting tired of it, which I can only say for a few other albums.<br /><br />I feel like what RFTI did (and did exceedingly well) was completely break from the medieval tropes of symphonic metal set in stone since the days of \\"Angels Fall First\\". RFTI is not bright and flowery, it is sparse in uplifting major chords, and it is not ornate with symphonic frills or attempts at operatics. It is dramatic, not in the way that a passion play or a love story about vampires and elves is dramatic, but in the way that a populist revolution or a hurricane is dramatic. It is dark, bleak, aggressive, and completely lacking in harpsichords. If you're mainly a fan of the old-school power-metal style of symphonic metal typical of Nightwish, Within Temptation, or the first couple of Epica albums even, you might be severely disappointed by RFTI. But if you're like me and have grown tired of the umpteenth symphonic metal release that does nothing but incrementally retread the same ground paved by Nightwish over a decade ago, this just might be the refreshing change of pace you were looking for.<br /><br />(Oh and one final thing--are people STILL complaining about Mark's vocals? It's been ten years for god's sake, and there's not been a single Epica album that doesn't feature Mark's vocals.  Moreover, death growls are basically bog standard in the metal genre, and complaining about it is like...complaining about alcohol in scotch whisky. Are you sure you're a drinker if this is such a big hangup for you?)

0062073567|5.0|So, I recently read Agatha Christie's The Murder Of Roger Ackroyd. If you haven't read it, you should really read it. It is pretty amazing. I was a little put off by the way that the narrator only has a personality that's filled in with very broad strokes. I'm used to detective novels where the narrator has a ness-ness-an essence-that permeates the whole book. In this one the main character, the doctor in a little village in the English countryside, doesn't really put a strong stamp on the novel. And Hercule Poirot, the detective, also doesn't really feel like he leaps off the page (although he is amusing). The most vivid characters (as in the average episode of Law and Order) are the side-characters. The doctor's gossipy sister is a real prize, I loved her. But also the various people around the manner are interesting, albeit archetypical.<br /><br />The real joy of the book is the plotting, though. This book has some of the meticulous plotting I've ever seen. It's like a machine. Once, I heard a writer talk about how the beginning of a novel should be filled with the people and complications that are going to propel the rest of the novel forward.<br /><br />Agatha Christie definitely follows this advice. She doesn't raise just one conundrum, she raises fifteen. How did this guy get murdered if the door was locked? Who murdered him if everyone has an alibi? Who was blackmailing his girlfriend? Who was his son talking to in the forest? Who does this mysterious wedding ring belong to? What's up with the anonymous phone call that alerted the doctor to his death? Who was the man who the doctor met as he was leaving the manor? The mysteries just pile higher and higher into this massive tottering edifice.<br /><br />And they don't get resolved one by one, either. Each time one mystery is answered, it adds two more. And with every suspect who gets eliminated, there's that much more pressure on each of the others. By the end, you feel like anyone can be the murderer. It was fantastic. I've never read anything like it.  And I highly recommend it.<br /><br />I think this is a perfect example of why all books don't necessarily need to have all things. The fact that this book had less of the typical joys of the detective novel (setting and personality) allowed me to enjoy its superlative plotting. In this case, I wouldn't say that it's thinness is at all a flaw. In fact, considering all the juggling that Christie is doing, I wonder if it would've even worked at all if the novel had been more filled with voice and details. I suspect that it would've failed.<br /><br />Crossposted from my blog: blotter-paper.coms fantastic. I've never read anything like it.  And I highly recommend it.<br /><br />I think this is a perfect example of why all books don't necessarily need to have all things. B007REV4YI|5.0|Being the year of the 50th anniversary of the beloved super spy, the 23rd Bond installment had a lot to live up to. While Casino Royale was a worthy and thrilling reboot to the franchise, Quantum of Solace fell flat with critics and fans. To make matters more interesting, MGM has had financial woes over the last number of years and needed a hit to reinvigorate the studio. Ladies and gentlemen, I give you Skyfall.<br /><br />Not only does the movie qualify as one of the most action-packed and entertaining movies of the year, it ranks on my list as one of the best Bond flicks to date. As has come to be expected, Craig is terrific as the British spy, portraying the character with charm and intensity. The action is top notch and the story is superbly written. Among my favorite moments were the blistering opening sequences, the introduction of the film's villain, and the intense final act, which finds Bond cornered yet unafraid. The real treat, however, is Bardem's role as the sinister Silva. Rivaling any villain in recent memory, save Heath Ledger's Joker, Bardem is intimidating, powerful, and irresistible to watch. You'll find yourself glued to the screen whenever he appears, and hoping he'll show up again soon when he's gone.<br /><br />Another huge nod has to go to Mendes, whose vision of Bond is spectacular. The movie is tightly scripted and the director does a very fine job of bringing out great performances from his cast. The cinematography is some of the best I've seen in a film for a long time; the production crew did a remarkable job utilizing light, colors, and atmosphere to really draw the audience in. Another treat is the glimpse the audiences gets into Bond's past, which plays a crucial role in the film.<br /><br />The transfer to Blu-ray is beautiful; the colors are pin-sharp and the picture is clear, crisp, and detailed. In a film that contains such vibrant colors and contrasts, this was immensely important for home release. All of the great cinematographic moments you saw in the theater look just as great here. The audio is equally perfect. The disc's DTS-HD MA 5.1 track contain all the intensity and clarity contained in the theatrical release so you can enjoy the crashes, explosions, gun shots, and dialogue in high definition.<br /><br />By way of special features, there's just under an hour of behind-the-scenes featurettes that are an interesting watch, as well as the original action-heavy trailer for the movie, \\"Skyfall Premiere\\" feature, and two different audio commentaries; a detailed and information-rich feature from Mendes and a not-so-interesting one from producers Barbara Broccoli and Michael G. Wilson and production designer Dennis Gassner. The insight from Mendes is fascinating and any fan of the film will be sure to enjoy it. The commentary from the producers felt more like a promotion of the film and could have been left off the disc in my opinion.<br /><br />Whether your a die-hard Bond fan or a casual observer, Skyfall has something for everyone. There are multiple references to past Bond adventures, yet enough new and innovative ideas to please newcomers to the franchise. I highly recommend this film. This BD set doesn't boast hours and hours of extra footage but it has enough to please fans that are curious about the making of the film. Overall a terrific movie, beautiful disc transfer, and engaging Blu-ray set.ic moments you saw in the theater look just as great here. The audio is equally perfect. B007REV4YI|5.0|The first thing I noticed about Skyfall was the vast amount of talent involved. With apologies to Sean Connery, Daniel Craig gives the best acting performances of any Bond. It's not that he's a better actor than Connery, but he's asked to play the role with a lot more realism, and that works well for me.<br /><br />Other actors of note are even better than Craig: Judi Dench is wonderful as M, Javier Bardem is over the top and hilarious as Silva, and Ralph Fiennes appears as Gareth Mallory. It's rare that Oscar winners and nominees have so many key roles in an action movie.<br /><br />In addition to the acting talent, Sam Mendes (American Beauty) directs, Thomas Newman (10 Oscar nominations) is responsible for the score, and Roger Deakins (9 Oscar nominations) does his usual excellent job with the cinematography.<br /><br />While I enjoyed Quantum of Solace, it was definitely flawed. It was too short, so everything felt rushed. It was also rather unbalanced, with five major chase scenes in the first 30 minutes, and an abundance of quick cuts and shaky-cam. Skyfall has a couple of major chase scenes, but the action never eclipses the overall story.<br /><br />The plot is pretty simple. A computer hard drive has been stolen, and it contains the identities and missions of MI6 agents. M's computer is hacked and she is informed that the identities of five agents will be released each week. It becomes clear that the knowledge originated from MI6, or from someone with intimate knowledge of the organization.<br /><br />Bond is injured and believed to be dead after an accident early in the movie, but he turns up alive when he feels that his services are needed. However, he's clearly not completely healthy. Is he fit for duty? Is age catching up with him? Would it be dangerous to allow him to resume duty while he is recovering? I like that Bond is portrayed as vulnerable. He can be hurt, and he can make mistakes.<br /><br />Craig's Bond is a different beast from any that have come before. He's colder and tougher, and the campy humor is gone. That's not to say that the humor is completely absent, it's just smarter and funnier. Many of the best lines are possible because of the relationship between Bond and M. Check out the word association scene for an example.<br /><br />Most of the expected elements are present. The movie visits China, Turkey, England and Scotland. There's obviously a new Bond girl, but she's in fewer scenes than you might expect. The music, action sequences and humor all add to the familiar feel. There's even a few references to previous Bond movies, and fans will love the inspired inclusion of an old piece of machinery.<br /><br />The movie also continues to establish characters that we have come to know and love from previous entries in the franchise, but I'll let you make those discoveries for yourself.<br /><br />After 23 movies and 50 years, the Bond franchise is alive and well. Many critics are suggesting that Skyfall is the best Bond movie to date. They may be right, but I can't decide between Skyfall and Casino Royale. Skyfall wouldn't work as well without the relationships and story arc established in Casino Royale and Quantum of Solace. The three Craig movies don't quite have the depth of the Bourne movies, but the gap is miniscule.<br /><br />Skyfall will appeal to fans of Bond, spy movies, and action movies. I only hope that Craig's next two appearances as Bond are up to this standard.<br /><br />Overall score 4.5/5come before. He's colder and tougher, and the campy humor is gone. B004V7XWEE|5.0|At a most definitive period in my life, Marvin Gaye delivered a single that so captured the times that it felt timeless and of its time that it felt like it was resonating from the inner depths of my soul. No song nor album could have been more descriptive of the formative time of graduating from high school and entering my first semester of college. I became enthralled with every lyric, every professional music turn by The Funk Brothers and, of course, Marvin's brilliant layering of vocals on top of vocals (a decade or so later Quincy Jones would emulate that production and vocal arrangement on the Michael Jackson \\"Off the Wall\\" and \\"Thriller\\" projects).<br /><br />The first single and title track felt autobiographical. I had just skirted a possible draft to Vietnam because the following year, 1972, the draft would end. That didn't stop my head from being obsessed with the potential horrors of that draft. It was the first, and pretty much the only war that was captured on American television everyday. (A couple of decades later during the Middle East crisis in Iraq, the nefarious rethuglican leadership fought to not have that war televised). The daily airing of the skirmishes and body bags invaded the evening news, along with political unrest and protest contributed greatly to the end of the draft, and eventually the end of the war. News reporters braved the circumstances and flew with various military units as we fought battles in real time on television. The American public had never seen anything quite like it. And Marvin, via firsthand accounts from his brother Frankie, wrote and sung about that terrible time. Despite the fact that Berry Gordy reportedly resisted releasing it because of its political leanings, the music was very Motown 70s. The original Motown Sound of the 60s had run its course. As the label grew from a powerhouse singles label in the 60s, the 70s meant an upgrade in the Motown sound and the production of one brilliant album after the other. Stevie's golden era began with \\"Where I'm Coming From\\", Diana's acclaimed solo debut also prepared the way for her breathtaking recording of the \\"Lady Sings the Blues\\" soundtrack, The Temptations had introduced Norman Whitfield's psychedelic soul that by 1971 perfectly blended their classic balladry with \\"The Sky's the Limit\\" and Smokey was temporarily detoured from launching his solo career as The Miracles experienced one final smash with \\"Tears of a Clown\\", though by 1973, he crafted the gorgeous solo debut simply entitled \\"Smokey\\". (It was a bit inaccurate about Motown solely being a singles labels in the 60s. The Supremes sold 50 million albums from 1964-1969, garnering 3 #1 albums and nearly a 4th. And The Temptations had no less than 5 Top 10-15 albums in the same time period, sharing a #1 with Diana Ross and The Supremes).<br /><br />Back to \\"What's Going On\\", the next track was a sequel of sorts \\"What's Happening Brother\\" which seemingly by osmosis, I yearn to hear right after the title track as the album invaded my soul. But, the album was released almost 6 months after the single. Hunger for more caused fans to instantly listen to side 2, a distinctively unique single mix of \\"God is Love\\" that was almost as significant as the title track. In less than 2 minutes, Marvin raised my spirituality with a wondrous lyric and summery musical sound.<br /><br />The 60s were times of great change, experimentation, celebration and hard lessons to learn. Discovering mind altering substances like the organic marihuana would also bring in hashish and man-manufactured drugs like LSD and more profound organic substances like heroin. What came out of the more well to do suburbs, began invading the city tenement housing with more street-tainted versions of these substances that made its way as far as to Vietnam. \\"Flying High in the Friendly Sky\\" captured that experience perfectly. Intriguing, frightening, but, always honest, it was possibly the most cinematic of all the songsMarvin crafted for \\"What's Going On\\". I felt as if I understood the allure of the first peaks that made those harder substances so powerful. But within 5 minutes, Marvin took the journey into its darkest corners.<br /><br />Just as we protest the war, the evolution of the women's liberation movement and the gay liberation movement, we were also aware of how disrespectful many of us had become of the environment. \\"Mercy, Mercy Me\\", the second single was one of the strongest anthems addressing the environment. Not quite as devastating as \\"Flying High in the Friendly Sky\\", \\"Mercy, Mercy Me\\" was no less arresting. Adapted by many environmental non-profits, it was a good song, written and sung with passion. It became his second Top 5 smash from the opus.<br /><br />After embracing my spirituality, the sequencing of \\"Save the Children\\" was very appropriate. I was always self-aware of the suffering around me, a lot of it because of fatherless homes, hardworking single mothers and children having to pay the price for something they did not create. Diana would combine it with \\"Brown Baby\\" the next year for one of the best medleys of its time. It also became the theme of one of the biggest festivals for healing our impoverished neighborhoods. Jesse Jackson spearheaded this festival, Motown provided the soundtrack and Marvin Gaye spearheaded a performance for the 1972 movie. Marvin sung passionately about our need to step outside ourselves and guide our children to a brighter tomorrow.<br /><br />The album version of \\"God is Love\\" closed out side two. The arrangement was slower and took awhile to get used to. Remember, I had lived with the \\"What's Going On\\" b/w \\"God is Love\\" single for several months. I eventually learned to appreciate both versions, though the single version still manages to out emote the album version. It was also descriptive of Marvin's heavy religious training. That was something very familiar to me as my family home was inundated with religious artifactsin nearly every room in our home. My mother placed a half size statue of the Virgin Mary, with the snake at her feet that gave me many chills on a given night. I could always reach for \\"God is Love\\" for solace.<br /><br />Side 2 opens up with the very urban snapshot of \\"Right On\\". After President Johnson's \\"Great Society\\" tried to address the staggering inner city poverty that was rising from the large tenement dwellings, Marvin captured the energy of the community to try and fight back against the beauracy. The inner city struggles collided with our awareness of self and community. As I often tried to explain, the black community from 1964-1970 morphed first from the somewhat condescending \\"colored\\" to the more distinguished but often misunderstood \\"negro between 1965-68, until James Brown gave identity to our burgeoning self pride in 1968 with \\"Say it Loud, I'm Black and I'm Proud\\" and the \\"black\\" experience, finally into the edification of a large portion of the black man's history by 1970's declaration of \\"African-American\\". Out of those profound changes came the affirmation, \\"Right On\\" that Marvin seemed to understand thoroughly.<br /><br />As the pain and pleasure of that era came full circle, Marvin would again hold onto his religious upbringing underscored by his new identity as a \\"spiritualist\\". None other than the fractured blend of religion and spirituality led to the awakening in \\"Wholy, Holy\\". It is a battle that would continue to resonate years to come as his next opus, \\"Let's Get it On\\" into \\"I Want You\\" would show his struggles between. The carnal and the spiritual. Musically, \\"Wholy Holy\\" often felt like a moment of grace. Aretha would record it the next year for her iconic \\"Amazing Grace\\" album. That alone meant that no artist blended his still evolving lessons from the more structured awareness to the defining confines of judgment.<br /><br />In closing, \\"Inner City Blues\\" was the final frontier. As much as I wished it all had a tidy ending, Marvin again reflected on the challenges that at times seem to show us a brighter path, but, too often it also demonstrated just how much negativity there would be on the journey to serenity. It scripted the confusion. Marvin tapped into his jazz-leanings. Marvin could write a love song so stirring as \\"If This World Were Mine\\" to the plaintive relaying in \\"Inner City Blues\\". It is no surprised that many contemporary jazz artists would cover this song for years to come. \\"inner City Blues\\" did indeed tell you \\"What's Going On\\". But it wasn't a sweet, tidy package that could be sourced at a moment's whim. He sung the lyrics through the lens of honesty.<br /><br />Subsequent editions of this monumental masterpiece, the final album recorded at Hitsville, the final album when all The Funk Brothers would be as one and one of the final Motown/Detroit releases. Marvin took the bold step of performing the entire album at the Kennedy Center in his hometown of Washington D.C. It was brilliant. He decided not to perform the album in its original sequence. It was a bit jarring after living with the album on a daily basis. So as I expected to hear a given song upon completion of the one before it, the live experience simply opened up another way to listen to this body of work.<br /><br />Marvin spent countless hours on this project though he described the experience with ease. The 40th Anniversary brings together all elements of the work he had cultivated over a two year period. There are a number of instrumentals that were never crafted into full songs with lyrics. You get a sense that he was living in an extended moment in God somewhere in time and space.<br /><br />So many so called musicologists wanted to relegate Motown to a singles label, but, in reality, as the 70s began Stevie was crafting his golden period of albums beginning with 1971's \\"Where I'm Coming From\\", Diana had immersed herself in Lady Day's catalog to come up with the sorely underrated #1 album, \\"Lady Sings the Blues\\" after crafting her brilliant Ashford & Simpson debut, The Temptations guided by Norman Whitfield changed the sound of music with \\"Cloud Nine\\", Smokey was a bit delayed in crafting \\"Quiet Storm\\" though his self-titled debut was no sleeper. In fact, Motown welcomed the 70s with high quality albums that continue to resonate a near half century later. Diana proved no fluke with the excellent Adult Contemporary, \\"Touch Me in the Morning\\" album, Marvin began his soul deep albums from this masterpiece to the misunderstood, \\"In My Lifetime\\" as his Columbia debut bristled with wild, new haunting sounds.........graduating in 1971, \\"What's Going On\\" proved to be quite revealing for my classmates and I and I began to buy almost any album on Motown, as the label graduated from great records to classic albums!

1594203334|5.0|Parents wanting to raise their kids thoughtfully and learn from different cultures will likely find this book helpful and entertaining. It's a memoir by an American expatriate mother living in France, documenting her investigation of French parenting.<br /><br />It's not that I necessarily agree with everything here. I don't think that's the point. The idea isn't that the French do parenting perfectly or that everyone should thoughtlessly copy them. Rather, the point is that while some French parenting practices may seem foreign to Americans, many of them really make sense and are very helpful and amazingly successful. And there is some clear evidence of better results.<br /><br />Unlike many parenting books and memoirs, this book is actually written in a skillful, fun and fresh way. It is intimate and emotional but not overly sentimental either. Even though it describes a mom's perspective, dads can enjoy it too! In fact, even if you never have had, and never will have, any children, it's unlikely you'll find this dull!

But for it's size, the Hyper 212+ a great performer and I am glad purchased this to replace the stock fan.

0060873175|2.0|The best Crichton stories put the reader into a time and place they have never thought about, and flesh that world out with mountains of data, images, sights, sounds, and trivia.<br /><br />The Great Train Robbery put the reader in Victorian England. Timeline in the high middle ages. Sphere underwater.  Even Pirate Latitudes, which was a silly, silly story, makes you feel like you're living and breathing in the Caribbean English colony during the romantic age of pirates.  He can't write a female character to save his live but he can write a good story.<br /><br />With Micro, you can see where Crichton was going.  Start with the outline of Jurassic Park, place those characters in an insect's world instead of a dinosaur's, throw in an evil corporation hell-bent on playing in God's domain (a recurring theme in later Crichton books, Pirate Latitudes notwithstanding) and see what happens.<br /><br />And it could have been awesome.<br /><br />I doubt that Crichton would have wanted the story to get published as it did.  As it stands, this is not even a mediocre book, and is all the worse because it could have been one of his best.  Life as a one-inch tall hunk of meat in a rain forest of insect predators is a fascinating concept, as anyone who has watched the movie Microcosmos can attest to (and I suspect that's where he got the idea for this).  I also believe he would have expanded that part of the story beyond just two days (like he did with Timeline) and probably put in more cooperation between the different camps of humans that got shrunk.  The philosophizing about the nature of nature that made it into the story also most likely came directly from Crichton, as it's similar to ideas he brought up in Andromeda Strain and Congo.<br /><br />What got published, however, is awful. Back in the 1950's and 1960's, that's how Bond was imagined by his creator.  Go back to the books - start with Casino Royale or From Russia with Love - and you will see that Bond is a tough, loyal, hard-edged spy.  Daniel Craig brings this kind of Bond back.  Sean Connery was glamorous, Roger Moore was silly, but Daniel Craig acts, moves, and thinks like a spy.<br /><br />And the villain in \\"SkyFall\\" was once an important asset of MI6...perhaps a friend of Bond once.  He is the enemy today because he wants revenge. Why? He was cruelly betrayed by Britain and M when it was expedient for him to get captured and tortured.  He was traded for 6 other British agents. See the film \\"The Spy Who Came in From the Cold\\" to get an idea how governments will and do make these choices at the personal sacrifice of their agents.  When my father was a spy against the Nazis for the French this was a real fear when he operated in enemy territory. Can you really trust your own side, when you live in the shadows?  And so Raoul Silva (Javier Barden) - the new enemy of MI6 - was born by surviving his betrayal. Governments do create their own Frankensteins.<br /><br />Both the flawed psychological, hurt natures of Bond and Silva are explored in this brilliant thriller. He has to use his brains, not only his courage, to win this showdown.  I love the part when he goes back into time, and drives his \\"Goldfinger\\" vehicle again.  The gadget that villian Raoul Silva uses, is one we live with everyday - the computer.  For he is an expert in cybercrime.<br /><br />Now we have reached Bond at 50 - Daniel Craig nails it. Although I must confess that this Bondis someone who doesn't smile or enjoy luxury wines - something I miss from the Sean Connery days.

0812992784|4.0|Just as Gregor Samsa found himself turned into a bug one fine morning in Franz Kafka's  'Metamorphosis', Salman Rushdie  finds himself sentenced to death on Valentine's day, 1989 by an Iranian Ayatollah's 'Fatwa'. This book, though an autobiography, primarily  deals with the horrendous ten years from 1989 onwards  as he lived  like a fugitive, evading assassination by the Islamic extremists. Adopting the pseudonym 'Joseph Anton', he is constantly on the move, protected by the Special Branch of Police. He is forced to trade privacy, freedom and choice in return for security. Though overwhelmed and in despair during the early years of such 'confinement', Salman Rushdie heroically fights back gradually to continue working on his books, falling in love and out of it, be a father, lover and a successful author. His chronicle of those ten years is a story of his battle for the freedom of speech and for the freedom of Art to express itself fearlessly. This memoir is at once evocative, honest and very heart-warming.<br /><br />The years under 'protection' was a mixed bag. On the one hand, Rushdie is immensely thankful to the splendid policemen who protected him round the clock . He is grateful to his friends in the literary and publishing world, who formed a 'ring of security' around him so that he could continue to live as normal a life as was possible. He is grateful to the Labor party govt which eventually gave him back his freedom. On the other hand, the upper echelons of the British Conservative Party, the Tory  govt, Scotland Yard and parts of the media like 'The Independent' had little sympathy for him and felt that he wasn't worth the money and effort expended in protecting him. They thought that he brought it on himself and should face the consequences. One senior officer in the Special Branch of Police told Rushdie that he hadn't served the nation the way other VIPs had done,  for whom such levels of protection is provided. Even the Christian church in the UK attacked him for hurting the sentiments of the Faithful. The antipathy of the Tories may be due to Salman being a lifelong Laborite but it is also possible  that race played a negative role here because it is hard to imagine similar lack of empathy to a writer like Martin Amis or Christopher Hitchens or Graham Greene. Finally, it was the Labor govt's Foreign Secretary Robin Cook and Derek  Fatchett who worked extremely hard with Iran to nullify the fatwa and give him back his freedom.<br /><br />What was Salman's Rushdie's 'crime'? It is appropriate here to recount the reasons for the fatwa of death on him. Rushdie , as an irreligious man and an atheist, says about the Prophet's Revelations: \\" ..a revealed text should be scrutinised like any other text, using all the tools of the critic, literary, historical, psychological, linguistic and sociological. In short, the text should be regarded as a human artefact and like all such artefacts, prey to human fallibility and imperfection\\". From this viewpoint, he goes on to analyse the early pronouncements of polytheism by the Prophet to the later switch to monotheism as expounded in the Quran. From what I understand, it is this approach based on recorded history and social context that has landed him in such major strife, some 1400 years after the Revelations. One can even understand the Ayatollah being outraged. But why should  British liberals and  the Left too? After all, this approach is rational and the Left believes in it as much as Rushdie does!<br /><br />The one really tragic event of the `fatwa years' for Rushdie was his apology to the Islamic fundamentalists for hurting their feelings.  The continuous stress of living under a `death threat' gets the better of him and he decides in a moment of despair and confusion,  to meet with some Egyptian clerics in London to negotiate the lifting of the `fatwa' by expressing regret for his action. He writes an apology to Ali Khameini of Iran regretting \\"profoundly the distressthe publication has occasioned to the sincere followers of Islam and living as we do in a world of many faiths, this experience has served to remind us that we must all be conscious of the sensibilities of others.\\" Rushdie regrets this action  deeply in his memoir because it was against whatever he stood for. It must have been very hard for an irreligious atheist to admit being a religious Muslim.<br /><br />On his personal life, Rushdie is very honest about his four broken marriages. He does not come off well in the way his first three marriages ended. He himself admits that his fourth wife, the supermodel Padma Lakshmi, paid him back in his own coin by breaking his heart and ending their marriage even though he still  wanted to be with her. The irony was that she left him for an even older man after complaining repeatedly to Rushdie that he was `too old' for her! However, Rushdie comes off as a devoted father to his two sons, Zafar and Milan.<br /><br />Another poignant event of those ten years is his forced exile from India, land of his birth. Just as in Britain, the Indian politicians were terrified of an Islamic backlash  and were  the first to ban `Satanic Verses'. They also denied him a visa to come back `home' during those ten years. For me, as an Indian, it is such a sad and shameful turn of events. Rushdie eventually gets permission to visit India after 1999 and he comes back `home' to a tumultuous welcome from the civil society and the media. Rushdie touchingly writes on the occasion that he felt like dropping to his knees and kissing the earth as he landed in Delhi airport.<br /><br />Rushdie does not have much time for Pakistan, its military dictatorships and religious extremism. Sadly for him, his irreligious parents decide to move to Pakistan in their later years, citing curiously the reason of finding `good Muslim husbands' for their daughters. Rushdie writes that this sounds dubious at best and that his parents never came clean to him about the reason for leaving Bombay in favor of Karachi, Pakistan. However, I must point out a counter to this view from a Pakistani journalist, Ajaz Ashraf. Ashraf writes that Rushdie's  aunts (two of his mother's sisters) were married  to high-ranking Pakistanis. One of them, Colonel Majeed Malik, was close to Pakistan's founder Md. Ali Jinnah and had the dubious distinction of censoring and doctoring Jinnah's biography and making it a sanitised version. The other was Shahid Hamid, who played a key role in Field Marshal Ayub Khan's rise to power in the 1960s. Later on, Hamid went on to found the notorious Spy agency, ISI,  and worked under Gen.Zia-ul-Haq. The journalist Ashraf contends that Rushdie must have known that his family moved to Pakistan opportunistically  to benefit from relatives who were in positions of power there. Rushdie does not even mention any of these relatives in his memoir. So, there may be some truth in the claims. But I can understand Rushdie's reluctance to deal elaborately with the Pakistani side of his family because his own life simply has nothing to do with Pakistan.<br /><br />The memoir makes absorbing reading and is written straight from the heart. It is perhaps a tad long at 636 pages and at times feels a little repetitive. Some readers may feel that Rushdie drops too many names in his narrative but I didn't feel that way. Because of his special situation, it was only natural that he got to meeting high-profile leaders like Bill Clinton, Tony Blair, Klaus Kinkel and so on. Being an acclaimed British writer in English, it is also not unusual that he practically knew all the big names in the British Literary and Publishing world.<br /><br />It is an excellent memoir.the publication has occasioned to the sincere followers of Islam and living as we do in a world of many faiths, this experience has served to remind us that we must all be conscious of the sensibilities of others.\\" Rushdie regrets this action  deeply in his memoir because it was against whatever he stood for. 0670020745|5.0|Strokes can be severely debilitating.  Recovery is often slow and rarely full.  [[ASIN:0452295548 My Stroke of Insight: A Brain Scientist's Personal Journey]] is Dr. Jill Bolte Taylor's account of her own devastating stroke and the long road back to herself.  Because she is a brain research scientist, her observations and conclusions are objective and knowledgeable.  Because she is human, her revelations and insights are compassionate and hopeful.<br /><br />Not only does she provide crucially valuable advice for victims of stroke and those who interact with and care for them, but she also shares her personal discovery that the power to choose a state of spiritual peace appears to reside in the right hemisphere of the brain.  Her experiences during her stroke and eight years of recovery have led her to believe that each of us has the ability to access that peace.<br /><br />Originally, I checked out the library's copy to read for a book discussion group.  Her writing style is so conversational and easy to digest -- especially the scientific information, which she contains in two stand-alone chapters -- and her subject so important and compelling, that I bought a copy to keep for future reference.

When the story moves to Scotland, the palette changes to greens, blues and browns as the topography changes.  From what I can tell, this transfer does not include any IMAX images shown in some theaters.<br /><br />Like the video, the audio track is exemplary. B007J4T2G8|4.0|Based on all the hype about this book I anticipated another cheesy romance novel. I was pleasantly surprised to discover that this story had more substance than that. Sexuality, especially female sexuality, is a taboo new frontier in popular literature, but certainly worth exploration. I thought Fifty Shades Book 1 was good first attempt at discussing the sexual relationships of men and women in our culture from a literary position. The greatest downfall of this novel is that it was obviously edited and published as a \\"bang book\\". James' surprisingly intelligent commentary on the psychological and emotional factors of sex are frequently rushed or glossed over to get to the next detailed account of Ana and Christian's passionate encounters. I hope that the following books delve a little deeper into the development of their relationship and its meaning while maintaining the well written descriptions of their intense lovemaking. The topic of female sexuality and the physical relationships of men and women in our society today certainly merits literary discussion. B000UZNSWM|4.0|The Coldest Winter: America and the Korean War is a very good read and serves as a good starting point for anyone interested in the Korean War. It is not a complete history of the war as it is singularly focused on the bungled handling of the Korean War by General Douglas MacArthur and some of his subordinate commanders, most notably Ned Almond. Halberstam also neatly chronicles the errors of political leaders in the United States, North Korea, South Korea, China and the Soviet Union that led to the Korean War and continued it well past when the fighting became a useless stalemate.<br /><br />Halberstam neatly chronicles events on the ground in Korea but frustratingly spends long chapters away from the battlefield discussing events in China. If you're looking strictly for a military history of the war this is not the book for you. Nor is it the book for you if you're looking for a balanced portrayal of General MacArthur, Ned Almond and MacArthur's intelligence officer Charles Willoughby. The way Halberstam tells this story MacArthur was not only incompetent but delusional as well and guys like Almond and Willoughby should have been court martialed and jailed for their incompetence. Halberstam does support each portrayal with a long litany of facts and incidents, but one does kind of get a feeling that, like all good writers, in order to \\"hook\\" a reader in to a story he needed a villain and he found several. After MacArthur's recall by President Truman the book moves quickly through the rest of the war.<br /><br />The end of the book is, in my opinion, a disaster. It felt like Halberstam didn't know where to end it so he just kept writing. The reader is treated to chapters on the two Koreas since the Korean War and chapters on Kennedy, Johnson and Vietnam that seem out of place. Halberstam should have cut those chapters out and ended with the chapter that discusses the experience since the war of some of the veterans Halberstam interviewed for the book. The quotes by veterans who lived through each battle detailed by Halberstam are what really make this book worth reading and the fate of the surviving veterans is quite interesting.<br /><br />This is a good starting point to get interested in the Korean War but it should not be treated as a good general overall history of the war.veterans who lived through each battle detailed by Halberstam are what really make this book worth reading and the fate of the surviving veterans is quite interesting.<br /><br />This is a good starting point to get interested in the Korean War but it should not be treated as a good general overall history of the war.
1609611543|5.0|There's little I can add that you won't find detailed in the personal anecdotes of other _Wheat Belly_ reviews. Suffice it to say that I found this book credible, cogent, persuasive, and truly terrifying. I have two boys, 7 and 10, and if even half -- no. If even 20% of what Davis says in this book is accurate (and I suspect the number is closer to the 85-95% range), then the severity and significance of what we have been doing to our children and ourselves cannot be understated.<br /><br />I finished listening to the audio version of _Wheat Belly_ a few minutes ago. (The narration is quite good, by the way.) I wasn't even half-way through the book, though, before I was telling my wife all about it and we'd decided to start transitioning our children away from wheat. It won't be easy. We went low-carb a year ago, so the move is less drastic for us. But when you consider all of the snacks, pizza parties, school lunches, and everything else we'll have to train them to deal with...it's daunting to say the least.<br /><br />My boys have a hard time focusing in school. For that matter, I have a hard time focusing on work. We blow such things off. \\"Oh, they're just boys. Oh, it's just middle age.\\" But they also have serious allergies. They also come from family lines riddled with diabetes, heart disease, cancer, and so on. What's more important? Giving them the habits that will help them toward a healthier, longer, more productive life...or having them fit in with the tasty yet destructive habits of their peers. We rail against the dangers of drugs, never knowing that we have been feeding them one of the most insidious, deleterious, addictive drugs of all with every meal since they were old enough to chew.<br /><br />Dr. Davis has set my family on a difficult road, but I view it as a mission, perhaps even a quest, and the prize at the end is not to suffer the same terrible fates as my parents and grandparents. In a way, eliminating wheat is a bit like eliminating carbon emissions. Yes, it's hard. Yes, it costs more. But in the end, even if the \\"theory\\" of human-exacerbated climate change is dead wrong, what's the worst that could happen? We have cleaner air and a host of more efficient manufacturing technologies in play. Same thing here. At the very worst, wheat elimination will leave me leaner and healthier. It may not prevent cancer, stave off Alzheimer's, and thwart diabetes. Then again, as Dr. Davis so thoroughly details, just maybe it will.
